Title: AI chip challenger Groq eyes APAC expansion
URL: https://www.computerweekly.com/news/366633487/AI-chip-challenger-Groq-eyes-APAC-expansion
Time Published: 2025-10-24T07:00:00Z
Full Content:
BAIVECTOR - AdobeStock US startup Groq has been challenging semiconductor powerhouses Nvidia and AMD in the artificial intelligence (AI) chip race with a novel chip architecture that can perform inferencing tasks at breakneck speeds. Unlike graphics processing units (GPUs), which have become the industry standard for both AI training and inferencing, Groq‚Äôs chips ‚Äì dubbed language processing units (LPUs) ‚Äì are built specifically for inference, the process of running a trained AI model to get a response. But while GPUs rely on external memory to store the AI model, making them prone to performance bottlenecks, Groq‚Äôs system distributes AI models across thousands of chips, storing them directly in faster, on-chip memory with lower latency and higher energy efficiency. Groq CEO Jonathan Ross, a former Google engineer who worked on the cloud provider‚Äôs tensor processing unit (TPU), likened the company‚Äôs chip architecture to a ballet performance. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs like a ballet choreography, where everyone just knows where to be,‚Äù Ross told Computer Weekly during a trip to Singapore. ‚ÄúOur chips know when to accept the data,‚Äù he said. GPU systems, on the other hand, are unpredictable, added Ross, comparing them to showing up for an unscheduled meeting and having to wait. Groq‚Äôs focus on inference workloads was deliberate. ‚ÄúWe weren‚Äôt going to make customers‚Äô lives better by working on training, which is too expensive, uses too much energy, and requires too much effort to get the software stack to work, but inference was not a solved problem,‚Äù he said, adding that it‚Äôs also a much bigger problem with 10 to 20 times more compute being deployed for inferencing compared with training workloads. During a demonstration, Ross showed how an AI model running on the company‚Äôs LPUs generated a complex, multi-day travel itinerary in the blink of an eye from a voice prompt, responding to a flurry of edits and requests almost instantaneously. ‚ÄúWe‚Äôre dramatically faster than GPUs,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúNormally, people have to put a lot of work into streaming the audio. We actually generate the entire audio and then start playing. That‚Äôs how fast it is.‚Äù Groq‚Äôs compiler was designed before the chip, allowing it to onboard some of the largest AI models much faster than others. Ross said a trillion-parameter model was up and running in production on a Monday after it was released on a Friday. ‚ÄúWe actually spent less time compiling it than downloading it,‚Äù he quipped. And, with large AI models running faster and more efficiently, Ross believes there will be less need to use smaller models or fine-tune a large model, which could be quickly superseded by a newer foundation model that performs just as well. ‚ÄúIt has become largely about getting one of the larger models to run as inexpensively and as fast as possible, which is what we do,‚Äù he said. Groq‚Äôs work to speed up inferencing has attracted a developer following in the Asia-Pacific (APAC) region, where there are 45,000 registered developers on Groq‚Äôs platform in Singapore alone, with India having the second-highest number of developers globally. The company, which was founded in 2016, also claimed that usage of its application programming interface (API) increased from 17% to 36% in the past year, surpassing Amazon‚Äôs, citing a recent industry survey. To meet the rising demand for its chips and the GroqCloud service, the startup has expanded from one datacentre to 13 in the past 12 months. It is now planning to open its first datacentre in APAC later this year. While datacentre operators continue to grapple with the power and cooling demand of AI workloads, Groq‚Äôs air-cooled chips can be deployed in older facilities that don‚Äôt support the liquid-cooling requirements of power-hungry GPUs. ‚ÄúIn Finland, Microsoft was moving out of an Equinix datacentre that‚Äôs air-cooled because it‚Äôs useless to them, and as they moved out, we moved in,‚Äù said Ross. ‚ÄúThere‚Äôs an enormous amount of air-cooled datacentre space that others can‚Äôt use because GPUs have to be liquid-cooled.‚Äù The company also lets organisations with data sovereignty and security requirements deploy its chips on-premise through partnerships with datacentre hardware suppliers. On-premise customers can get their own custom region, and also use GroqCloud if they require more capacity than they bought, he said. Groq‚Äôs growth has been fuelled by strong financial backing from key investors. In September 2025, it raised $750m in a Series E funding round led by Disruptive, with participation from major investors like BlackRock, Samsung and Cisco, valuing the firm at $6.9bn.

But if young investors don√¢¬Ä¬ôt wake up to the patterns√¢¬Ä¬îhow fiat currency became a tool of debt enslavement, how stocks are manipulated by hedge funds, how real estate is hoarded by institutional buyers√¢¬Ä¬îthey√¢¬Ä¬ôll watch crypto suffer the same fate. The warning signs are already here. The Biden administration√¢¬Ä¬ôs 2021 infrastructure bill included crypto tax reporting requirements so onerous that even The Wall Street Journal called them unworkable. The European Union√¢¬Ä¬ôs MiCA regulations, while less draconian, still impose KYC (Know Your Customer) rules that erode privacy. And let√¢¬Ä¬ôs not forget the World Economic Forum√¢¬Ä¬ôs gleeful predictions of a cashless society where "you will own nothing and be happy." Crypto√¢¬Ä¬ôs original promise was decentralization. But if Gen Z trades financial freedom for the false security of government "protections," they√¢¬Ä¬ôll repeat the same mistakes their parents made√¢¬Ä¬îtrusting the very system that√¢¬Ä¬ôs fleecing them. The Bitcoin regulation reckoning The shift is happening. Swyftx√¢¬Ä¬ôs data shows that within two years, younger Australians may be just as likely to buy Bitcoin as traditional stocks. But momentum depends on two things: regulation and education. The first is a double-edged sword. Yes, clear rules could bring institutional money into crypto, stabilizing prices and reducing volatility. But as we√¢¬Ä¬ôve seen with every other asset class, regulation is a Trojan horse. Banks didn√¢¬Ä¬ôt enter the crypto space out of altruism; they did it to co-opt the competition. Morgan Stanley now offers Bitcoin exposure to its wealthy clients√¢¬Ä¬înot because it believes in decentralization, but because it wants to profit from the revolution it once dismissed. The second factor√¢¬Ä¬îeducation√¢¬Ä¬îis where the battle will be won or lost. Younger generations understand the basics of crypto, but do they grasp the deeper game? Do they know that the Federal Reserve√¢¬Ä¬ôs money-printing binges devalue their savings? Do they realize that the same politicians pushing "affordable housing" policies are often the ones taking donations from BlackRock and real estate lobbyists? Do they see the connection between central bank digital currencies (CBDCs) and China√¢¬Ä¬ôs social credit system, where dissenters can have their funds frozen with a keystroke? The answers will determine whether crypto remains a tool of liberation or becomes just another cog in the machine. Right now, the signs are mixed. On one hand, you have Gen Z traders making nearly $10,000 in profits on average√¢¬Ä¬îproof that they√¢¬Ä¬ôre learning to navigate the space. On the other, you have a generation that√¢¬Ä¬ôs disturbingly comfortable with surveillance, seemingly unaware that financial privacy and personal freedom are inseparable. The housing crisis wasn√¢¬Ä¬ôt an accident. It was the result of deliberate policies. Crypto offered an escape hatch, but the window is closing. Governments and banks are circling, ready to turn Bitcoin into just another regulated, taxed, and surveilled asset. The question for millennials and Gen Z is no longer about whether to invest. It√¢¬Ä¬ôs about whether they√¢¬Ä¬ôll fight to keep crypto free√¢¬Ä¬îor hand the keys to the same institutions that stole their future in the first place. Sources include: CoinTelegraph.com CoinTelegraph.com Enoch, Brighteon.ai This isn√¢¬Ä¬ôt hypothetical. It√¢¬Ä¬ôs the reality for millions of young Australians, Americans, and Westerners under 40 who watched Bitcoin climb from $400 to over $100,000 while they were told to trust the same institutions that inflated housing prices beyond reason. The question isn√¢¬Ä¬ôt just about missed opportunities anymore. It√¢¬Ä¬ôs about whether an entire generation will ever recover from a financial betrayal orchestrated by central banks, corrupt politicians, and a rigged real estate market√¢¬Ä¬îor if cryptocurrency, despite its risks, remains their only lifeline. Now, as governments scramble to regulate crypto, the same forces that locked young people out of homeownership are positioning themselves to control the one asset that could set them free. The irony is brutal. The system that failed them now wants to dictate how they use their last escape route. Key points: A decade of theft: Younger generations were sold the lie that student debt, low wages, and skyrocketing home prices were just "market realities"√¢¬Ä¬îwhile Bitcoin, dismissed as a gamble, surged 23,000% in the same period. The housing trap: Australia ranks as the sixth most expensive property market globally, with millennials and Gen Z now viewing crypto as their only shot at financial independence. Regulation as a double-edged sword: While proper oversight could legitimize crypto, history shows governments use "protections" to seize control√¢¬Ä¬îjust as they did with fiat currency, stocks, and real estate. The surveillance trade-off: A staggering 33% of Gen Z would accept government surveillance in their homes, revealing a dangerous naivety about how financial control leads to totalitarian overreach. The Bitcoin awakening: Younger investors are shifting from stocks to crypto, but will they recognize the deeper game√¢¬Ä¬îwhere banks and states manipulate every asset class to keep them dependent? How Big Banks and Big Government stole the dream For decades, the script was simple: go to college, get a job, buy a house, retire comfortably. But somewhere between the 2008 financial crisis and the COVID-era money printing, the script was rewritten√¢¬Ä¬îwithout telling the actors. Central banks slashed interest rates to near-zero, inflating asset bubbles that only the already-wealthy could access. Wages stagnated while corporate landlords and BlackRock snapped up single-family homes to turn them into rental cash cows. The result? In Australia, 40% of under-35s now say their biggest financial regret isn√¢¬Ä¬ôt splurging on avocado toast√¢¬Ä¬îit√¢¬Ä¬ôs ignoring Bitcoin when it was cheaper than a used car. The numbers don√¢¬Ä¬ôt lie. In 2015, Bitcoin traded between $172 and $465. Today, it hovers around $107,000. A $1,000 investment then would be worth over $2.3 million now. Meanwhile, the median home price in Sydney has surged past $1.4 million, with wages failing to keep pace. The message to young people is clear: the traditional paths to wealth are closed. The only question left is whether they√¢¬Ä¬ôll let the system close the alternatives, too. Swyftx√¢¬Ä¬ôs survey reveals the desperation. Eighty percent of Australians under 50 regret their financial decisions over the past decade. Crypto isn√¢¬Ä¬ôt just an investment for them; it√¢¬Ä¬ôs a rebellion. "Housing unaffordability at this scale is a predicament other generations didn√¢¬Ä¬ôt face," a Swyftx spokesperson told Cointelegraph. "Crypto is seen as an opportunity to get ahead." But here√¢¬Ä¬ôs the catch: the same institutions that priced them out of homes are now moving to regulate√¢¬Ä¬îand potentially strangle√¢¬Ä¬îcrypto. Australia√¢¬Ä¬ôs Labor Party has already proposed folding crypto exchanges under existing financial laws. On the surface, this sounds like "protection." In reality, it√¢¬Ä¬ôs the first step toward co-opting the one asset young people still control. The surveillance state√¢¬Ä¬ôs endgame There√¢¬Ä¬ôs a disturbing paradox in the data. While younger generations flock to crypto as a hedge against a broken system, a third of Gen Z would willingly invite government surveillance into their homes√¢¬Ä¬îostensibly to "monitor domestic violence." This isn√¢¬Ä¬ôt just ignorance; it√¢¬Ä¬ôs a failure to recognize how financial control morphs into total control. History shows that once governments gain a foothold in monitoring assets, they rarely stop there. Look at Canada√¢¬Ä¬ôs 2022 trucker protests, where Prime Minister Justin Trudeau froze protesters√¢¬Ä¬ô bank accounts without due process. Or the U.S. IRS√¢¬Ä¬ôs push to track transactions over $600, a move that would give the state real-time visibility into every citizen√¢¬Ä¬ôs spending. Crypto was supposed to be the exit ramp from this kind of overreach. But if young investors don√¢¬Ä¬ôt wake up to the patterns√¢¬Ä¬îhow fiat currency became a tool of debt enslavement, how stocks are manipulated by hedge funds, how real estate is hoarded by institutional buyers√¢¬Ä¬îthey√¢¬Ä¬ôll watch crypto suffer the same fate. The warning signs are already here. The Bitcoin regulation reckoning The shift is happening. Swyftx√¢¬Ä¬ôs data shows that within two years, younger Australians may be just as likely to buy Bitcoin as traditional stocks. But momentum depends on two things: regulation and education. The first is a double-edged sword. Yes, clear rules could bring institutional money into crypto, stabilizing prices and reducing volatility. But as we√¢¬Ä¬ôve seen with every other asset class, regulation is a Trojan horse. Banks didn√¢¬Ä¬ôt enter the crypto space out of altruism; they did it to co-opt the competition. Morgan Stanley now offers Bitcoin exposure to its wealthy clients√¢¬Ä¬înot because it believes in decentralization, but because it wants to profit from the revolution it once dismissed. The second factor√¢¬Ä¬îeducation√¢¬Ä¬îis where the battle will be won or lost. Younger generations understand the basics of crypto, but do they grasp the deeper game? Do they know that the Federal Reserve√¢¬Ä¬ôs money-printing binges devalue their savings? Do they realize that the same politicians pushing "affordable housing" policies are often the ones taking donations from BlackRock and real estate lobbyists? Do they see the connection between central bank digital currencies (CBDCs) and China√¢¬Ä¬ôs social credit system, where dissenters can have their funds frozen with a keystroke? The answers will determine whether crypto remains a tool of liberation or becomes just another cog in the machine. Right now, the signs are mixed. On one hand, you have Gen Z traders making nearly $10,000 in profits on average√¢¬Ä¬îproof that they√¢¬Ä¬ôre learning to navigate the space. On the other, you have a generation that√¢¬Ä¬ôs disturbingly comfortable with surveillance, seemingly unaware that financial privacy and personal freedom are inseparable. The housing crisis wasn√¢¬Ä¬ôt an accident. It was the result of deliberate policies. Crypto offered an escape hatch, but the window is closing. Governments and banks are circling, ready to turn Bitcoin into just another regulated, taxed, and surveilled asset. The question for millennials and Gen Z is no longer about whether to invest. It√¢¬Ä¬ôs about whether they√¢¬Ä¬ôll fight to keep crypto free√¢¬Ä¬îor hand the keys to the same institutions that stole their future in the first place. Sources include: CoinTelegraph.com CoinTelegraph.com Enoch, Brighteon.ai Key points: A decade of theft: Younger generations were sold the lie that student debt, low wages, and skyrocketing home prices were just "market realities"√¢¬Ä¬îwhile Bitcoin, dismissed as a gamble, surged 23,000% in the same period. The housing trap: Australia ranks as the sixth most expensive property market globally, with millennials and Gen Z now viewing crypto as their only shot at financial independence. Regulation as a double-edged sword: While proper oversight could legitimize crypto, history shows governments use "protections" to seize control√¢¬Ä¬îjust as they did with fiat currency, stocks, and real estate. The surveillance trade-off: A staggering 33% of Gen Z would accept government surveillance in their homes, revealing a dangerous naivety about how financial control leads to totalitarian overreach. The Bitcoin awakening: Younger investors are shifting from stocks to crypto, but will they recognize the deeper game√¢¬Ä¬îwhere banks and states manipulate every asset class to keep them dependent? How Big Banks and Big Government stole the dream For decades, the script was simple: go to college, get a job, buy a house, retire comfortably. But somewhere between the 2008 financial crisis and the COVID-era money printing, the script was rewritten√¢¬Ä¬îwithout telling the actors. Central banks slashed interest rates to near-zero, inflating asset bubbles that only the already-wealthy could access. Wages stagnated while corporate landlords and BlackRock snapped up single-family homes to turn them into rental cash cows. The result? In Australia, 40% of under-35s now say their biggest financial regret isn√¢¬Ä¬ôt splurging on avocado toast√¢¬Ä¬îit√¢¬Ä¬ôs ignoring Bitcoin when it was cheaper than a used car. The numbers don√¢¬Ä¬ôt lie. In 2015, Bitcoin traded between $172 and $465. Today, it hovers around $107,000. A $1,000 investment then would be worth over $2.3 million now. Meanwhile, the median home price in Sydney has surged past $1.4 million, with wages failing to keep pace. The message to young people is clear: the traditional paths to wealth are closed. The only question left is whether they√¢¬Ä¬ôll let the system close the alternatives, too. Swyftx√¢¬Ä¬ôs survey reveals the desperation. Eighty percent of Australians under 50 regret their financial decisions over the past decade. Crypto isn√¢¬Ä¬ôt just an investment for them; it√¢¬Ä¬ôs a rebellion. "Housing unaffordability at this scale is a predicament other generations didn√¢¬Ä¬ôt face," a Swyftx spokesperson told Cointelegraph. "Crypto is seen as an opportunity to get ahead." But here√¢¬Ä¬ôs the catch: the same institutions that priced them out of homes are now moving to regulate√¢¬Ä¬îand potentially strangle√¢¬Ä¬îcrypto. Australia√¢¬Ä¬ôs Labor Party has already proposed folding crypto exchanges under existing financial laws. On the surface, this sounds like "protection." In reality, it√¢¬Ä¬ôs the first step toward co-opting the one asset young people still control. The surveillance state√¢¬Ä¬ôs endgame There√¢¬Ä¬ôs a disturbing paradox in the data. While younger generations flock to crypto as a hedge against a broken system, a third of Gen Z would willingly invite government surveillance into their homes√¢¬Ä¬îostensibly to "monitor domestic violence." This isn√¢¬Ä¬ôt just ignorance; it√¢¬Ä¬ôs a failure to recognize how financial control morphs into total control. History shows that once governments gain a foothold in monitoring assets, they rarely stop there. Look at Canada√¢¬Ä¬ôs 2022 trucker protests, where Prime Minister Justin Trudeau froze protesters√¢¬Ä¬ô bank accounts without due process. Or the U.S. IRS√¢¬Ä¬ôs push to track transactions over $600, a move that would give the state real-time visibility into every citizen√¢¬Ä¬ôs spending. Crypto was supposed to be the exit ramp from this kind of overreach. But if young investors don√¢¬Ä¬ôt wake up to the patterns√¢¬Ä¬îhow fiat currency became a tool of debt enslavement, how stocks are manipulated by hedge funds, how real estate is hoarded by institutional buyers√¢¬Ä¬îthey√¢¬Ä¬ôll watch crypto suffer the same fate. The warning signs are already here. The Biden administration√¢¬Ä¬ôs 2021 infrastructure bill included crypto tax reporting requirements so onerous that even The Wall Street Journal called them unworkable. The European Union√¢¬Ä¬ôs MiCA regulations, while less draconian, still impose KYC (Know Your Customer) rules that erode privacy. And let√¢¬Ä¬ôs not forget the World Economic Forum√¢¬Ä¬ôs gleeful predictions of a cashless society where "you will own nothing and be happy." Crypto√¢¬Ä¬ôs original promise was decentralization. But if Gen Z trades financial freedom for the false security of government "protections," they√¢¬Ä¬ôll repeat the same mistakes their parents made√¢¬Ä¬îtrusting the very system that√¢¬Ä¬ôs fleecing them. The answers will determine whether crypto remains a tool of liberation or becomes just another cog in the machine. Right now, the signs are mixed. On the other, you have a generation that√¢¬Ä¬ôs disturbingly comfortable with surveillance, seemingly unaware that financial privacy and personal freedom are inseparable. The housing crisis wasn√¢¬Ä¬ôt an accident. It was the result of deliberate policies. Crypto offered an escape hatch, but the window is closing. It√¢¬Ä¬ôs about whether they√¢¬Ä¬ôll fight to keep crypto free√¢¬Ä¬îor hand the keys to the same institutions that stole their future in the first place. Sources include: CoinTelegraph.com CoinTelegraph.com Enoch, Brighteon.ai The numbers don√¢¬Ä¬ôt lie. In 2015, Bitcoin traded between $172 and $465. Today, it hovers around $107,000. The message to young people is clear: the traditional paths to wealth are closed. The only question left is whether they√¢¬Ä¬ôll let the system close the alternatives, too. It√¢¬Ä¬ôs about whether they√¢¬Ä¬ôll fight to keep crypto free√¢¬Ä¬îor hand the keys to the same institutions that stole their future in the first place. Sources include: CoinTelegraph.com CoinTelegraph.com Enoch, Brighteon.ai Look at Canada√¢¬Ä¬ôs 2022 trucker protests, where Prime Minister Justin Trudeau froze protesters√¢¬Ä¬ô bank accounts without due process. Or the U.S. IRS√¢¬Ä¬ôs push to track transactions over $600, a move that would give the state real-time visibility into every citizen√¢¬Ä¬ôs spending. Crypto was supposed to be the exit ramp from this kind of overreach. But if young investors don√¢¬Ä¬ôt wake up to the patterns√¢¬Ä¬îhow fiat currency became a tool of debt enslavement, how stocks are manipulated by hedge funds, how real estate is hoarded by institutional buyers√¢¬Ä¬îthey√¢¬Ä¬ôll watch crypto suffer the same fate. The warning signs are already here. The Biden administration√¢¬Ä¬ôs 2021 infrastructure bill included crypto tax reporting requirements so onerous that even The Wall Street Journal called them unworkable. The European Union√¢¬Ä¬ôs MiCA regulations, while less draconian, still impose KYC (Know Your Customer) rules that erode privacy. And let√¢¬Ä¬ôs not forget the World Economic Forum√¢¬Ä¬ôs gleeful predictions of a cashless society where "you will own nothing and be happy." Crypto√¢¬Ä¬ôs original promise was decentralization. But if Gen Z trades financial freedom for the false security of government "protections," they√¢¬Ä¬ôll repeat the same mistakes their parents made√¢¬Ä¬îtrusting the very system that√¢¬Ä¬ôs fleecing them. The Bitcoin regulation reckoning The shift is happening. Swyftx√¢¬Ä¬ôs data shows that within two years, younger Australians may be just as likely to buy Bitcoin as traditional stocks. But momentum depends on two things: regulation and education. The first is a double-edged sword. Yes, clear rules could bring institutional money into crypto, stabilizing prices and reducing volatility. But as we√¢¬Ä¬ôve seen with every other asset class, regulation is a Trojan horse. Banks didn√¢¬Ä¬ôt enter the crypto space out of altruism; they did it to co-opt the competition. Morgan Stanley now offers Bitcoin exposure to its wealthy clients√¢¬Ä¬înot because it believes in decentralization, but because it wants to profit from the revolution it once dismissed. The second factor√¢¬Ä¬îeducation√¢¬Ä¬îis where the battle will be won or lost. Younger generations understand the basics of crypto, but do they grasp the deeper game? Do they know that the Federal Reserve√¢¬Ä¬ôs money-printing binges devalue their savings? Title: Bitcoin Poised To Top $1.5 Million, Says Ex-PayPal President
URL: https://bitcoinist.com/bitcoin-top-1-5-million-ex-paypal-president/
Time Published: 2025-10-24T00:00:34Z
Full Content:
In a wide-ranging Coin Stories interview published October 21, David Marcus‚Äîco-founder and CEO of Lightspark, former president of PayPal and co-creator of Diem, the cryptocurrency project initiated by Facebook‚Äîlaid out a thesis that Bitcoin will ultimately surpass gold in value and evolve from a purely ‚Äústore-of-value‚Äù asset into the invisible, neutral settlement layer of the internet for money. ‚ÄúI think Bitcoin will be more valuable than gold,‚Äù Marcus told host Natalie Brunell. ‚ÄúAt today‚Äôs gold price, it‚Äôs, you know, $1.3 million a bitcoin that [it] will have a higher market cap than gold.‚Äù He emphasized he is ‚Äúdecent at directional predictions‚Äù but ‚Äúterrible at timing,‚Äù framing the trajectory as inevitable over a five-to-ten-year horizon rather than a near-dated call. The implication of outgrowing gold‚Äôs market capitalization places Bitcoin‚Äôs potential value well north of $1.3 million per coin and, at Gold‚Äôs recent ATH at $4,381.58/oz, above $1.53 million. Marcus‚Äôs price view is inseparable from his broader contention that BTC must progress beyond a narrow ‚Äúdigital gold‚Äù narrative. Echoing a line from analyst Matt Pines that Brunell cited‚Äî‚Äúif Bitcoin is only a store of value, it has failed‚Äù‚ÄîMarcus agreed ‚Äúfully,‚Äù but added a critical caveat: the savings-asset phase was a necessary precondition for utility. ‚ÄúWe believe that the store-of-value phase is absolutely essential for us to be able to actually build a utility phase of Bitcoin on top of it,‚Äù he said, arguing that institutional adoption, ETFs and nation-state accumulation conferred enough legitimacy to begin scaling real-world payments. ‚ÄúNow that every institution‚Ä¶ whether it‚Äôs BlackRock, Fidelity or others, are actually supporting Bitcoin‚Ä¶ we can actually really start building payment utility on top of it.‚Äù That utility vision hinges on using Bitcoin more like TCP/IP‚Äîan invisible settlement substrate‚Äîthan as a volatile unit of account for everyday spending. Marcus was blunt about behavioral and economic constraints: ‚ÄúPeople don‚Äôt want to use Bitcoin for everyday purchases because it‚Äôs volatile and people want to actually benefit from appreciation‚Ä¶ they don‚Äôt want to be the Bitcoin pizza guy.‚Äù Lightspark‚Äôs approach is to move fiat end-to-end while using BTC in the middle. ‚ÄúYou can send dollars from a US bank account to someone in Mexico receiving Mexican peso‚Ä¶ the settlement asset is Bitcoin in between. So you have dollars, Bitcoin, Mexican peso and it‚Äôs invisible to people using it.‚Äù Technically, Lightspark is pushing beyond Lightning‚Äôs channel model while remaining backward-compatible. Marcus lauded Lightning‚Äôs trust model and speed, but highlighted its liquidity and self-custody frictions when scaled to ‚Äúbillions of endpoints.‚Äù The company‚Äôs newly launched ‚ÄúSpark‚Äù is described as a Lightning-compatible, non-channel payment system that enables spinning up ‚Äúbillions of wallets‚Äù with ‚Äúminimal new trust assumptions.‚Äù Crucially, he said, it preserves safety valves: ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs not as trustless as Lightning, but we believe it‚Äôs trustless enough and has unilateral exits to Layer 1‚Ä¶ you can pull a rip cord and no one can prevent you from recovering your funds on L1.‚Äù Marcus also argued that stablecoins‚Äîdespite their centralized issuer model‚Äîare an unavoidable component of global payments, and that anchoring them to BTC‚Äôs settlement layer enhances resilience. He described a personal ‚Äúschizophrenic journey‚Äù with stablecoins, disliking the ‚Äúsingle throat to choke,‚Äù yet accepting their ubiquity and attempting to minimize trust by avoiding separate gas tokens and preserving unilateral exits to Bitcoin L1. On adoption, Marcus pointed to shifting institutional sentiment. Recounting a panel in New York ‚Äúput together by Citadel Securities,‚Äù he said a ‚Äúmajority‚Äù of a roughly 450-person room of large traditional-finance investors indicated they own BTC, versus far fewer holding ETH, stablecoins, or any token. ‚ÄúThis is a room that traditionally would have been very resistant to Bitcoin‚Ä¶ now the times have changed so profoundly.‚Äù Yet he still framed retail penetration as early, estimating ‚Äúlow hundreds of millions‚Äù of unique holders globally and significant headroom from there. Overall, Marcus‚Äô thesis returns to first principles: BTC as neutral, scarce, programmable collateral and a credibly decentralized settlement layer. Dismissing critiques that it lacks ‚Äúintrinsic value,‚Äù he argued, ‚ÄúUnderlying scarcity of Bitcoin secured by code is the intrinsic value‚Ä¶ this is the only thing that‚Äôs deflationary by nature.‚Äù That, he contends, is why Bitcoin should outcompete gold over time: ‚ÄúWhen the first gold ETFs were launched, they started mining more gold. You can‚Äôt do that with Bitcoin.‚Äù If and when that market-cap crossover arrives, it would validate the structural call embedded in his remarks‚Äîand, by extension, the headline-grabbing notion that BTC‚Äôs fair value is not just above seven figures, but ultimately ‚Äúmore valuable than gold,‚Äù which today maps to $1.5 million. At press time, BTC traded at $109,060. For updates and exclusive offers enter your email. Jake Simmons has been a Bitcoin enthusiast since 2016.
